Exemptions from Partnership in Mufawadah
Ruling on exemptions from partnership in mufawadah via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on which purchases are exempt from partnership in a mufawadah contract and explain the role of istihsan in deriving this exception.

Core Idea
Food, clothing, and condiments for the family are exempt from partnership by istihsan due to necessity.

In a mufawadah partnership, the general rule is that all purchases are jointly owned. However, certain everyday household necessities are treated as personal expenses. This lesson explores the exception for food, clothing, and condiments, and the legal reasoning (istihsan) that justifies it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and the exception
Read the matn: 'Whatever each one of them purchases will be in partnership except the food of his family and their clothing, and likewise condiments.'
General rule: all purchases are joint. Exception: food, clothing, condiments for family.
2
Understand the basis of the general rule
The contract requires equality and mutual agency; each partner acts on behalf of the other, so purchases are shared.
Mufawadah means full partnership in wealth and liability.
3
Recognize the role of istihsan
The exception is not based on qiyas (analogy) but on istihsan (juridical preference), which overrides qiyas due to necessity.
Istihsan is a valid Hanafi source of law.
4
Analyze the necessity argument
Family needs are recurring and unavoidable. It is impractical to impose these on the partner or to account for every purchase.
Necessity (ḍarūrah) is the illa for the exception.
5
Note the cross-madhhab implication
Other schools may not accept istihsan and may treat all purchases as joint.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَا يَشْتَرِيْهِ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا تَكُونُ عَلَى الشَّرَكَةِ إِلَّا طَعَامَ أَهْلِهِ وَكِسْوَتَهُمْ وَكَذَا الْإِدَامُ لأَنَّ مُقْتَضَى الْعَقْدِ المُسَاوَاةَ وَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا قَائِمُ مَقَامَ صَاحِبِهِ فِى التَّصَرُّفِ وَكَانَ شِرَاءُ أَحَدِهِمَا كَشِرَائِهِمَا إِلَّا مَا اسْتَتَنَاهُ فِي الْكِتَابِ وَهُوَ اسْتِحْسَانُ لِأَنَّهُ مُسْتَنى عَنِ الْمُفَاوَضَةِ لِلضَّرُورَةِ فَإِنَّ الْحَاجَةَ الرَّانِيَةَ مَعْلُومَةُ الوقوع وَلَا يُمْكِنُ إِيجَابُهُ عَلَى صَاحِبِهِ وَ لَا الصَّرْفُ مِنْ مَّالِهِ وَ لَا بُدَّمِنَ الشَّرَاءِ فَيَخْتَصُّ بِهِ ضَرُورَةً وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يَكُونَ عَلَى الشَّرُكَةِ لِمَا بَينا
He said: Whatever each one of them purchases will be in partnership except the food of his family and their clothing, and likewise condiments; because the requirement of the contract is equality, and each one of them stands in the place of his partner in disposition, and the purchase of one of them is like the purchase of both, except what he excluded in the book, and it is istihsan, because it is excepted from mufawadah by necessity. Indeed, the recurring need is known to occur, and it is not possible to make it obligatory upon his partner, nor to spend from his wealth, and purchase is unavoidable, so it is exclusively his by necessity. Analogy would require it to be in partnership, as we explained.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof by istihsan. The matn states that all purchases by a mufawadah partner are jointly owned except household necessities: food, clothing, and condiments for the family. The commentary explains that while the general rule requires equality and mutual agency, these items are excluded by istihsan (juridical preference) due to necessity. Analogy (qiyas) would include them in the partnership, but necessity overrides because each person's family needs are personal, cannot be imposed on the partner, and must be purchased. Therefore, they are personal expenses, not shared.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juridical preference that overrides analogy due to necessity, custom, or equity.
ضرورت (ḍarūrah): Necessity that provides an exception to the general rule.
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A type of partnership where each partner has full agency and liability equal to the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are food and clothing for the family exempt from partnership in mufawadah?
A) Because they are minor expenses
B) Because they are personal necessities that cannot be shared or imposed on the partner
C) Because the partner does not eat the same food
D) Because the contract explicitly excludes them

Key Takeaway
Family necessities are exempt from partnership in mufawadah by istihsan due to necessity.

Shared and Non-Shared Debts in Mufawadah
Ruling on which debts are shared between mufawadah partners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which debts are shared between mufawadah partners and state the principle determining liability.

Core Idea
Only debts from valid partnership transactions are shared; personal or tort debts are not.

In a mufawadah partnership, partners are equal in capital, profit, and liability. However, not every debt incurred by one partner becomes a joint obligation. The law distinguishes between debts arising from the partnership's permitted transactions and those from personal or criminal matters. This lesson clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The passage states that debts incurred as a substitute for something in which partnership is valid are shared (the partner is a guarantor). This establishes a principle: only debts from transactions that can be jointly undertaken are joint.
2
List examples of valid partnership transactions
The text mentions purchase, sale, and hiring as transactions where partnership is valid. Debts arising from these (e.g., price of goods bought on credit, hire charges) are shared between partners.
3
List examples of invalid partnership transactions
The text lists jināyah (criminal injury), nikah (marriage), khul‘ (divorce by compensation), sulh ‘an dam al-‘amd (compromise for intentional homicide), and nafaqah (maintenance). These are personal obligations, not sharable.
4
Understand the rationale
Partnership liability is limited to transactions that can be jointly performed. Personal acts like marriage or crimes are outside the scope of the partnership, so debts from them remain individual.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether a debt is joint or personal by checking if the underlying transaction is valid for partnership.
Test yourself with the practice tasks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَايَلْزَمُ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِنَ الدُّيُون بَدَلَا عَمَّا يَصِحُ فِيهِ الْإِشْتِرَاكُ فَالَّا خَرُضًا مِنْ لَهُ تَحْقِيفًا لِلمَسَاوَاةِ فَمَا يصح فيه الإشتراك الشَّرَاءُ وَالْبَيْعُ وَالْإِسْتِجَارُ وَ مِنَ الْقِسْمِ الاخر الجِنَايَةِ وَالنِّكَاحُ وَالحُلْعُ وَالصُّلح عَنْ دَمِ العَمَدِ وَعَنِ النَّفَقَةِ
He said: And whatever debts are incurred by each one of them as a substitute for that in which partnership is valid, then the other is a guarantor for it, to achieve equality. So that in which partnership is valid includes purchase, sale, and hiring. And from the other category (where partnership is not valid) are: injury (homicide), marriage, khul', and sulh for deliberate blood (compromise for homicide), and regarding maintenance.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement categorizing debts that are shared between mufawadah partners. Debts arising from transactions where partnership is valid—like buying, selling, and hiring—are shared because the partner is a guarantor. Debts from invalid partnership transactions—like those from criminal acts (diyat), marriage (mahr), khul' (divorce by compensation), compromise for intentional homicide, and maintenance (nafaqah)—are not shared. The commentary elaborates with examples: if one partner commits a tort, the other is not liable; if a female partner takes khul', the male partner is not liable for the compensation.

Key Terms
جنایت (jināyah): Criminal act causing harm, leading to liability for diyat or other compensation.
خلع (khulʿ): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation.
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance owed to family members.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following debts would be shared between mufawadah partners?
A) Diyat for manslaughter committed by one partner
B) Mahr of a wife married by one partner
C) Price of goods bought on credit for the partnership business
D) Khul‘ compensation paid by a female partner

Key Takeaway
Only debts from transactions valid for mufawadah partnership are joint; personal, tort, and matrimonial debts remain individual.

intermediate
6 min
Seller's Right and Partner's Recourse in Mufawada
States the ruling on seller's right to claim price from any partner and partner's right of recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seller's right to claim the price from any mufawada partner and explain the paying partner's right of recourse from partnership assets.

Core Idea
In mufawada, each partner is a surety, so the seller can claim from any partner, and the paying partner recovers from the partnership.

In a mufawada (unlimited partnership), each partner is both an agent and a surety for the others. This unique feature affects third-party rights. This lesson examines the seller's right to claim the purchase price from any partner and the internal recourse among partners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the partnership type
The ruling applies specifically to mufawada, an unlimited partnership where partners are equal and mutually sureties.
Contrast with 'inan partnership where suretyship is not automatic.
2
State the seller's right
The seller can claim the price from the buyer or from any other partner, at his choice.
The buyer is liable as the direct contractor; other partners are liable as sureties.
3
Explain the basis: kafala
In mufawada, each partner is a kafil (surety) for the others in trade-related debts.
This is a distinguishing feature of mufawada.
4
Analyze the paying partner's recourse
After paying, the surety partner can recover from the other partner's share from the common property.
The amount recovered is proportionate to the other partner's share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ اور مفاوضہ کے شرکاء میں سے اگر کسی نے کوئی چیز خریدی تو اس کے فروخت کرنے والے کو یہ حق ہوگا کہ صرف خرید نے والے سے نہیں بلکہ شرکاء میں سے جس سے جی چاہے اس کی قیمت وصول کرلے خریدار سے اس لئے کہ وہی اصل معاملہ کرنے والا ہے اور شرکاء سے مطالبہ کا اس لئے حق ہو گا کہ وہ اس کے کفیل ہوتے ہیں۔ اور کفیل اس کی قیمت ادا کر دینے کے بعد اپنے دوسرے ساتھی سے اس کے حصہ کے مطابق اس کی بقیہ قیمت وصول کرلے ۔ کیونکہ ساتھی پر جو قرض تھا وہ دوسرے نے اپنے آپس کے مشترک مال سے ادا کیا ہے۔
Translation: If any of the mufawada partners buys something, the seller has the right to recover the price not only from the buyer but from any partner he wishes. From the buyer because he is the one who conducted the transaction, and from the partners because they are his sureties (kafils). And after the surety pays the price, he can recover from his other partner the remaining amount proportionate to his share, because the debt that was upon the partner was paid by the other from their joint common property.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage states a ruling specific to mufawada (unlimited partnership). In mufawada, each partner is both an agent (wakil) and a surety (kafil) for the others in all trade-related transactions. Therefore, if one partner buys goods for the partnership, the seller can claim the price from any partner because each is a surety. The partner who pays then has the right to recover from the others according to their shares from the partnership assets, since the debt was paid from common property. This ensures equality and mutual responsibility.

Key Terms
مفاوضہ (mufāwaḍah): An unlimited partnership where partners are equal in capital, agency, and surety, sharing all profits and losses of trade.
کفیل (kafīl): A surety who becomes liable for the debt of another; in mufawada, each partner is a kafil for the others in trade debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In mufawada partnership, if one partner buys goods on credit, from whom can the seller claim the price?
A) Only from the buyer partner
B) Only from the other partner
C) From any partner he chooses
D) Only from the partnership fund

Key Takeaway
In mufawada, the seller can claim from any partner, and the paying partner has the right to recoup from the partnership assets proportionately.

Scope of Mutual Liability in Mufawada Partnership
Defines shared and personal debts in mufawada partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which debts are shared in a mufawada partnership and state the legal basis for the distinction.

Core Idea
In mufawada, only debts from trade transactions are shared; personal liabilities like crime or marriage remain individual.

This lesson examines a key rule of mufawada partnership: mutual surety for debts arising from valid partnership transactions. The matn lists purchase, sale, and hire as shared, while crimes, marriage, khul', and maintenance are not. Understanding this boundary preserves the commercial nature of the partnership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing principle
The debt must arise from a transaction in which partnership (ishtirak) is valid. Determine if the underlying transaction is commercial and permissible for shirkah.
If the transaction is not valid for partnership, the debt is personal.
2
List transactions in which partnership is valid
According to the text, purchase (shira'), sale (bay'), and hire (ijarah) are valid. Debts from these are shared.
Both valid and corrupt sales are included, but the amount differs.
3
List transactions in which partnership is NOT valid
Crimes (jinayah), marriage (nikah), khul', compromise for intentional homicide, and maintenance (nafaqah) are not valid for partnership. Debts from these are personal.
These are personal to the individual partner.
4
Apply the rule to valid vs. corrupt sale
In a valid sale, the surety is for the agreed price. In a corrupt sale (e.g., missing a condition), the surety is for the market value at the time of contract.
The commentary provides this detail to clarify the extent of liability.
5
Understand the rationale
Mutual surety in mufawada aims to maintain equality (musawat) between partners in commercial dealings. Personal matters have no trade purpose, so they remain individual.
This keeps the partnership focused on trade.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَايَلْزَمُ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِنَ الدُّيُون بَدَلَا عَمَّا يَصِحُ فِيهِ الْإِشْتِرَاكُ فَالَّا خَرُضًا مِنْ لَهُ تَحْقِيفًا لِلمَسَاوَاةِ فَمَا يصح فيه الإشتراك الشَّرَاءُ وَالْبَيْعُ وَالْإِسْتِجَارُ وَ مِنَ الْقِسْمِ الاخر الجِنَايَةِ وَالنِّكَاحُ وَالحُلْعُ وَالصُّلح عَنْ دَمِ العَمَدِ وَعَنِ النَّفَقَةِ
ترجمہ اور جس چیز میں شرکت صحیح ہو اس کے عوض ان مفاوضہ کرنے والوں کے درمیان جو بھی قرض کسی ایک پر لازم ہوگا اس کا دوسر! ساتھی بھی لازمی طور سے ضامن اور ذمہ دار ہوگا۔ تا کہ دونوں میں مساوات باقی رہے۔ پس وہ معاملات جن میں شرکت کرنا صحیح ہے وہ میں خرید و فروخت کرنا، کرائے پر لینا دینا اور دوسری قسم کی وہ چیزیں جن میں شرکت صحیح نہ ہو وہ یہ ہیں جنایت قتل وغیر ہ اور نکاح مضلع قتل عمد کے بعد صلح اور نفقہ صلح وغیرہ ان میں سے خرید و فروخت کی صورت تو ظاہر ہے۔ لیکن بیچ صحیح میں قیمت متعینہ کا ضامن ہوگا۔ یعنی جو قیمت ان کے درمیان طے ہو چکی ہو اور بیع فاسد میں اس کی بازاری قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا یعنی بازار میں جو اس کی قیمت ہو اور اجارہ لینے کی صورت یہ ہے کہ مفاوضہ کے دونوں شرکاء میں سے کسی ایک نے اس تجارت کے کام میں کوئی مزدور یا جانور یا دوسری کوئی سواری یا چیز کرایہ پر لی تو کرایہ پر دینے والے کو اختیار ہوگا کہ اپنا کر یہ دونوں ساتھیوں میں سے جس کسی سے بھی چاہے اس سے وصول کر لے۔ کیونکہ تجارت کے کام میں ہر ایک دوسرے کا نفیل اور ذمہ دار ہے اور کاروباری معاملہ اور مقصد کے سوا اگر کسی دوسرے کا ان میں سے کسی پر کچھ بھی لازم آجائے تو وہ اسی کے ساتھ مخصوص رہے گا۔
He said: Whatever debts are incurred by each of them as a substitute for something in which partnership is valid, the other partner is a surety for him, to establish equality. What partnership is valid in: purchase, sale, and hire. And from the other category: crimes, marriage, khul', compromise for intentional homicide, and maintenance. Translation: And whatever is incurred in exchange for something in which partnership is valid, among the mufawada partners, any debt that becomes due on one, the other partner is also necessarily surety and responsible, so that equality remains. So the transactions in which partnership is valid are: purchase, sale, hire. And the other category in which partnership is not valid are: crimes like murder, marriage, khul', compromise after intentional homicide, and maintenance, etc. Among these, the case of purchase and sale is obvious. But in a valid sale, he is surety for the specified price, i.e., the price agreed between them; in a corrupt sale (bai' fasid), he is surety for its market value. And the case of hire is that if one of the two mufawada partners hires a worker, animal, or other conveyance for the purpose of trade, the lessor has the option to recover his rent from whichever of the two partners he wishes, because in matters of trade each is the surety and responsible for the other. And aside from business transactions and objectives, if something else becomes due on any of them, it remains confined to that person.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage from the matn defines the scope of mutual liability in mufawada partnership. The principle is that only debts arising from transactions in which partnership is valid (sahih al-ishtirak) are shared. These are purchase, sale, and hire (ijarah) – all commercial activities. In these, each partner is a surety for the other, ensuring equality. The commentary explains that in a valid sale, the surety is for the agreed price; in a corrupt sale (e.g., lacking a condition), the surety is for the market value because the contract is not fully sound. For hire, the lessor can claim from any partner. In contrast, debts from crimes, marriage, khul', compromise for intentional homicide, and maintenance are not shared because these are personal to the individual partner and not part of the trade. The commentary provides examples: if a partner commits a crime, only he is liable; if a woman partner takes khul' from her husband, her co-partner is not liable for the compensation; similarly for a partner's wife's mahr or compromise for intentional homicide. This distinction preserves the commercial nature of mufawada.

Key Terms
شِراء و بَیع (shirā’ wa bay‘): Purchase and sale are commercial transactions valid for partnership and thus shared debts in mufawada.
جنایت (jināyah): Crime, such as murder or injury; not a commercial matter so not shared in mufawada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following debts is shared among mufawada partners?
A) A partner's obligation to pay mahr to his wife
B) A partner's liability for accidentally injuring someone
C) A partner's debt for hiring a worker for the partnership business
D) A partner's payment in khul' with his wife

Key Takeaway
In mufawada, only debts from commercial transactions (purchase, sale, hire) are shared; personal obligations remain individual.

Kafala bi al-Mal by a Mufawada Partner: Binding Effect on the Other Partner
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the binding effect of a partner's guarantee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whether one mufawada partner's guarantee for a stranger binds the other partner, and explain the reasoning based on the dichotomy of tabarru' and mu'awada.

Core Idea
A guarantee by a mufawada partner may bind the other partner if it ends as compensation, not if it remains pure donation.

In a mufawada (unlimited partnership), partners are agents and sureties for each other. But does a unilateral guarantee given by one partner for a third party create a binding obligation on the other partner? This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), focusing on the nature of kafala bi al-mal as either a pure donation (tabarru') or a transaction that becomes compensatory (mu'awada) by its end. The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is whether the non-guaranteeing partner becomes liable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
One partner becomes a guarantor (kafil) for money on behalf of a stranger (ajnabi). The key question: does this kafala bind the other partner under the mufawada contract?
The act is kafala bi al-mal, not kafala bi al-nafs.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that it binds the other partner (when done by the order of the principal). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that it does not bind.
The Sahibayn consider it a pure donation (tabarru') which should not be included in mufawada.
3
Examine the dalil of each
Abu Hanifa argues that although it starts as a donation, it becomes compensatory (mu'awada) after payment because the guarantor gains a right of recourse against the principal. The Sahibayn argue it is purely gratuitous from beginning to end, as shown by its invalidity from minors and its restriction to one-third from the sick.
Abu Hanifa uses the principle: 'Consider the end result.'
4
Recognize the condition
Both parties agree that if the guarantee was given without the order of the principal, it does not bind the partner. The absolute statement in the matn is interpreted as restricted by this condition.
The condition is essential; without it, the meaning of mufawada (mutual agency) is absent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the guarantee binds, the other partner's assets may be used to fulfill the guarantee. If it does not bind, only the guarantor's own share is liable.
This is the practical difference that affects partnership liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَو كَفَلَ أحَدُهُمَا بِمَالٍ عَنْ أَجْنَبِي إِرَمَ صَاحِبَهُ عِندَ أَبِي حَيْقَة وَقَالَا لَا يَلْزَمُهُ لِأَنَّهُ تَبَرُّعُ وَلِهَدَ الابْصِحُ مِنَ الصَّبِي وَالعَبْدِ الْمَادُونِ وَالْمُكَابِ وَلَوْ صَدَرَ مِنَ الْمَرِيضِ يَصِحُ مِنَ التَّلْتِ وَصَارَ كَالْإِقْرَاضِ وَالْكَفَالَةِ بِالنَّفْسِ وَلا بِي حَنِيْفَةً أَنَّهُ تَبَرُّعُ ابْتِدَاءً وَمُعَاوَضَةً بَقَاءً لِأَنَّهُ يَسْتَوِجِبُ الضّمَانَ بِمَايُؤَدِّى عَلَى الْمَكْفُولِ عَنْهُ إِذَا كَانَتِ الْكَفَالَةُ بِأَمْرِهِ فَبِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى الْبَقَاءِ يَتَضَمَّنُهُ الْمُفَاوَضَةُ وَبِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى الْإِبْتِدَاءِ لَمْ تَصِحُ مِمَّن ذَكَرَهُ وَيَصِحُ مِنَ الثُّلْتِ مِنَ الْمَرِيضِ بِخِلَافِ الْكَفَالَةِ بِالنَّفْسِ لأَنَّهُ تَبَرُّعُ ابْتِدَاءً وَأَنْتِهَاء وَأَمَّا الْإِقْرَاضُ فَعَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ صَاحِبَهُ وَ لَوْ سُلَّمَ فَهُوَإعَارَةً فَيَكُونُ لِمِثْلِهَا حُكْمُ عَيْنِهَا لَا حُكْمُ الْبَدَلِ حَتَّى لَا يَصِحُ فِيهِ الْأَجَلُ فَلَا يَتَحَقَّقَ مُعَاوَضَةٌ وَلَوْ كَانَتْ بِغَيْرِ أمْرِهِ لَمْ تَلْزَمْ صَاحِبَهُ فِى الصَّحِيحِ لِانْعِدَامٍ مَعْنَى الْمُفَاوَضَةِ وَمُطَلَقُ الْجَوابِ فِى الْكِتَابِ مَحْمُول عَلَى الْمُقَيَّدِ وَضِمَانُ الْعَصَبِ وَالإستهلاك بِمَنْزِلَةِ الكَفَالَةِ عِنْدَ بِي حَنِيفَةً لِأَنَّهُ مُعَاوَضَةٌ انْتِهَاءٌ
He said: If one of them becomes a guarantor (kafil) for money on behalf of a stranger, it binds his partner according to Abu Hanifa, but they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it does not bind him because it is a donation (tabarru'). And [by analogy] it is not valid from a minor, a slave who is permitted [to trade], and a mukatab. If it occurs from a sick person, it is valid from one-third. And it becomes like a loan and guarantee of the person (kafala bi al-nafs). According to Abu Hanifa, it is a donation at the beginning but a compensation (mu'awada) at the end, because he becomes entitled to indemnity for what he pays from the principal (makful 'anhu) if the guarantee was by his order. So with regard to the end, it is included in the mufawada; with regard to the beginning, it is not valid from those mentioned. And it is valid from the sick person's third, unlike guarantee of the person because that is donation from start to end. As for a loan (iqrad), from Abu Hanifa it is transmitted that it binds his partner. But if it is conceded, then it is a loan (i'ara) and its ruling is the ruling of the thing itself, not of the substitute, so that fixing a term is not valid in it, and compensation is not realized. If it is without his order, it does not bind his partner in the correct view, because the meaning of mufawada is absent. And the absolute statement in the book is interpreted as restricted. And guarantee of usurpation (daman al-ghasb) and consumption (istihlak) are on the same footing as guarantee (kafala) according to Abu Hanifa, because it is compensation at the end.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of mutual surety in mufawada. The matn states that if one partner becomes a guarantor (kafil) for money on behalf of a stranger (who is not a partner), Abu Hanifa holds that this guarantee binds the other partner as well, whereas the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, considering it a pure donation (tabarru') which should not be binding on the other partner. The Sahibayn support their view by noting that such a guarantee is not valid from a minor, a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun), or a mukatab (slave on contract of manumission) because they cannot validly donate. If a sick person gives such a guarantee, it is valid only from one-third of his estate, like other donations. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that a guarantee by money (kafala bi al-mal) is a donation at its inception but becomes a compensatory transaction (mu'awada) at its end: when the guarantor pays the debt, he gains a right of recourse against the principal debtor (makful 'anhu). Thus, considering the end, the mufawada partnership encompasses it. However, considering the beginning, it is not valid from minors etc. The commentary then clarifies that this only applies if the guarantee was given by the order of the principal; if without order, it does not bind the partner. The text also distinguishes guarantee of the person (kafala bi al-nafs), which remains a pure donation throughout and thus never binds the other partner. The passage further discusses a loan (iqrad) from a partner: according to Abu Hanifa, it binds the partner, but if conceded as a loan (i'ara), it is like lending a thing itself, not a substitute (badal), so no compensation is realized; thus it does not create a binding obligation on the partner. The ruling is conditioned on the principal's order. The absolute statement in Quduri is interpreted as restricted by that condition. Finally, guarantee for usurpation (daman al-ghasb) and consumption are similar to kafala in Abu Hanifa's view because they end in compensation.

Key Terms
کفالت (kafālah): Guarantee; a contract where one person becomes liable for a debt or obligation of another.
تبرع (tabarru‘): A gratuitous act, donation, or voluntary transfer without compensation.
معاوضہ (mu‘āwaḍah): Compensatory transaction where each party gives and receives value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does a mufawada partner's guarantee for money (kafala bi al-mal) for a stranger bind the other partner?
A) Because it is a pure donation at both beginning and end
B) Because it starts as compensation and ends as donation
C) Because it ends as a compensatory transaction (mu'awada) giving right of recourse
D) Because it is identical to a loan

Key Takeaway
The binding effect of a mufawada partner's guarantee on the other partner depends on whether the guarantee is tabarru' (not binding) or mu'awada (binding), and on the condition of the principal's order.

Effect of Inheriting Partner-able Property on Mufawada
States that inheriting partnership-able property invalidates mufawada, converting to 'inan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of one partner inheriting partner-able property on a mufawada contract and explain the underlying legal reasoning (musawa condition and conversion to 'inan).

Core Idea
Inheriting partner-able property breaks mufawada; the contract converts to 'inan, which does not require equality.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a specific scenario in partnerships: what happens when a mufawada partner receives exclusive property that could be included in the partnership. Since mufawada requires continuous equality in capital, such receipt invalidates the mufawada. However, the contract is not entirely void; it transforms into a valid 'inan partnership. We will examine the condition of musawa, the conversion mechanism, and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly triggers the change? One partner inherits or receives a gift of property that can be subject to partnership. The ruling is that the mufawada becomes invalid (batilah).
Only property that can be partnership capital (e.g., money, goods) triggers this; non-partner-able property (e.g., usufruct) does not.
2
Locate the condition that is lost
Mufawada requires equality (musawa) in capital immediately and continuously. The newly acquired property belongs exclusively to one partner, breaking equality.
Equality is a condition (shart) for the validity of mufawada, not merely a recommended element.
3
Understand the non-sharing rationale
The other partner does not share in the inherited property because there is no legal cause (sabab) for him to have a claim. Unlike jointly earned profits, inheritance is an individual acquisition.
If both partners had jointly earned, cause would exist, and the property would be shared.
4
Note the conversion to 'inan
The contract is not completely void; it converts to an 'inan partnership. This is possible because 'inan does not require equality in capital.
The conversion reflects the principle that a contract is upheld as far as possible ('ikhtilaf al-‘aqd la yubtiluh').
5
Recognize non-binding nature
Mufawada is not a binding (lazim) contract, so it can be dissolved without penalty. The continuous state is treated like initiation: each partner may withdraw.
If mufawada were binding, dissolution would require mutual consent or court order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ فَإِنْ وَرِثَ أَحَدُهُمَا مَا لَا يَصِحُ فِيهِ الشَّرْكَةُ أَو وُهِبَ لَهُ وَوَصَلَ إلى يَدِهِ بَطَلَتِ الْمُفَاوَضَهُ وَصَارَتْ عَنَانَاً لقوات المساواة فِيمَا يَصْلَحُ رَأسَ الْمَالِ إِذْهِيَ شَرطُ فِيهِ ابْتِدَاءَ وَبَقَاءَ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ الْاخَرَ لَا يُشَارِكُمْ فِيمَا أَصَابَهُ لانْعِدَامِ السَّبَبِ فِي حَقِهِ إِلَّا أَنَّهَا تَنْقَلِبُ عَنَانًا لِلْإِمْكَانِ فَإِنَّ الْمُسَاوَاةَ لَيْسَ بِشَرِطٍ فِيْهِ وَلِدَوَامِه حُكْمُ الْإِبْتِدَاءِ لِكُونِ، غَيْرَ لازم
He said: If one of them inherits property in which partnership is not valid, or it is gifted to him and reaches his hand, the mufawada is invalidated and becomes 'inan due to loss of equality in what can serve as capital, for equality is a condition in it initially and continuously. This is because the other does not share with him in what has come to him due to absence of cause in his regard, except that it becomes 'inan because it is possible, for equality is not a condition in it, and its continuous state has the ruling of initiation because it is not binding.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn (likely al-Hidayah) on what happens when a mufawada partner receives property that can be included in the partnership through inheritance or gift. Since mufawada requires equality in capital at both start and continuation, receiving such property exclusively breaks equality, so the mufawada contract becomes invalid and converts into a 'inan partnership (a limited partnership where equality is not required). The reasoning is that the other partner cannot share in this new property because there is no legal cause (such as joint acquisition) for him to have a share. The conversion to 'inan is permissible because 'inan does not require equality. The final phrase notes that mufawada is not a binding contract (lazim), so its dissolution is permissible.

Key Terms
عنان ('inan): A limited partnership where partners share in profits but not necessarily in all dealings; equality is not required.
مساواة (musawa): Equality of partners in capital and authority, a condition for the validity of mufawada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to a mufawada partnership when one partner inherits money that can be used as capital?
A) The partnership remains valid as is
B) The mufawada is invalidated and becomes 'inan
C) The inherited money must be shared equally
D) The partnership is dissolved entirely

Key Takeaway
Acquiring exclusive partner-able property in a mufawada breaks the condition of equality, invalidating the mufawada but converting it to an 'inan partnership.

Inheriting Non-Partner-able Property Does Not Affect Mufawada
Ruling on inheritance in mufawada with reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that inheriting 'arad or 'aqar does not invalidate mufawada and explain the reason based on the unsuitability of such property for partnership.

Core Idea
Inheriting property not suitable for partnership does not break mufawada equality.

In a mufawada partnership, partners must contribute equal capital that is suitable for trade. This passage clarifies an important exception: if one partner inherits non-fungible goods or real estate, the partnership is not disrupted because such assets cannot be counted as partnership capital. The inheritor keeps it personally, preserving the required equality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the scenario described? A partner in mufawada inherits 'arad (non-fungible goods) or 'aqar (real estate). The ruling: the inherited property is his alone, and the mufawada is not invalidated.
2
Locate the reason (ta'lil)
The passage gives the cause: 'because partnership is not valid in it, so equality is not required.' This means 'arad and 'aqar are not considered proper capital for shirkah in the Hanafi school.
The illa is the unsuitability of the inherited property for partnership.
3
Understand the condition of equality
Mufawada requires equality (musawa) in the capital contributed. Since these items cannot form part of the partnership capital, they do not affect the equality condition. The inheritor simply retains them outside the partnership.
This is an application of the general rule that only partnership-suitable assets are subject to the equality condition.
4
Contrast with inheriting partnership-able property
If the inherited asset were money or goods suitable for trade, it would normally need to be included in the partnership capital, potentially disrupting equality and invalidating the mufawada. The passage implicitly highlights this contrast.
The ruling is specific to non-partnership-able assets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ وَرِتَ أَحَدُهُمَا عَرَضًا فَهُوَلَهُ وَلَا تَفْسُدُ الْمُقَاوَضَةُ وَكَذَا الْعَفَارُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَصِحُ فِيهِ الشَّرْكَةُ فَلَا يَشْتَرِطُ المساواة فيه
If one of them inherits a non-fungible good, it is his alone, and the mufawada is not invalidated. Likewise for real estate, because partnership is not valid in it, so equality is not required in it.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage provides an exception to the previous rule. When a mufawada partner inherits 'arad (non-fungible goods) or 'aqar (real estate), the partnership does not become invalid because such items are not ordinarily subject to partnership in the Hanafi school (they are not considered suitable as trading capital). Since mufawada only requires equality in capital that can be used for trade, inheriting such items does not disrupt the condition of musawa. Therefore, the partnership remains intact. This ruling highlights the Hanafi distinction between capital that is suitable for shirkah (e.g., money, commodities) and property that is not (e.g., land, personal belongings).

Key Terms
عرض ('arad): Non-fungible property, like household goods, that is not typically used as capital in partnerships.
عقار ('aqar): Real estate, immovable property.
مفاوضة (mufawada): A type of partnership where partners contribute equal capital and have equal rights and responsibilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The house must be shared, breaking the partnership
B) The house belongs to the inheritor alone, and the partnership continues
C) The partnership becomes 'inan
D) The house is sold and proceeds shared

Key Takeaway
Inheriting property that cannot serve as partnership capital does not invalidate a mufawada; the inheritor keeps it personally and equality is preserved.

6 min
Effect of Inherited Property on Mufawadah Partnership
States rulings on inheritance causing mufawadah to become 'inan or remain valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how inheriting or receiving partnership-eligible property affects a mufawadah partnership, and distinguish between property types that do and do not trigger transformation into 'inan.

Core Idea
Equality in partnership-eligible capital is a perpetual condition for mufawadah; its loss transforms the contract into 'inan, but not for goods or real estate.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario in partnership law: what happens when one partner suddenly acquires new wealth through inheritance or gift? The ruling depends on the nature of the acquired property. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of mufawadah, a partnership form unique to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct situations: (A) the inherited/gifted property is of a type in which partnership is valid (i.e., currency), and (B) the property is of a type in which partnership is not valid (goods or real estate).
Partnership in 'arad (goods) and 'iqar (real estate) is invalid according to Hanafi law.
2
Apply the condition of musawah
Mufawadah requires equality (musawah) in the capital that forms the basis of the partnership. This condition applies at inception and must persist throughout the partnership.
Capital must be of a type that can serve as partnership property.
3
Analyze the first scenario
If the acquired property is partnership-eligible (currency), the other partner cannot share in it due to lack of cause (sabab). Equality is lost, so mufawadah becomes void as mufawadah but transforms into 'inan because 'inan does not require equality.
The contract continues, but with the legal regime of 'inan.
4
Analyze the second scenario
If the acquired property is 'arad (goods) or 'iqar (real estate), partnership is not valid in such items. Therefore, equality in them is not a condition, and the mufawadah remains completely valid and unchanged.
The property belongs solely to the recipient partner and does not affect the partnership.
5
Summarize the key distinction
The determining factor is whether the acquired property could have been partnership capital. If yes, the loss of equality transforms the partnership; if no, the partnership is unaffected.
This reflects the principle that mufawadah requires musawah in what is capable of being partnership property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ فَإِنْ وَرِثَ أَحَدُهُمَا مَا لَا يَصِحُ فِيهِ الشَّرْكَةُ أَو وُهِبَ لَهُ وَوَصَلَ إلى يَدِهِ بَطَلَتِ الْمُفَاوَضَهُ وَصَارَتْ عَنَانَاً لقوات المساواة فِيمَا يَصْلَحُ رَأسَ الْمَالِ إِذْهِيَ شَرطُ فِيهِ ابْتِدَاءَ وَبَقَاءَ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ الْاخَرَ لَا يُشَارِكُمْ فِيمَا أَصَابَهُ لانْعِدَامِ السَّبَبِ فِي حَقِهِ إِلَّا أَنَّهَا تَنْقَلِبُ عَنَانًا لِلْإِمْكَانِ فَإِنَّ الْمُسَاوَاةَ لَيْسَ بِشَرِطٍ فِيْهِ وَلِدَوَامِه حُكْمُ الْإِبْتِدَاءِ لِكُونِ غَيْرَ لازم . فَإِنْ وَرِثَ أَحَدُهُمَا عَرَضًا فَهُوَ لَهُ وَلَا تَفْسُدُ الْمُفَاوَضَةُ وَكَذَا الْعِقَارُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَصِحُ فِيهِ الشَّرْكَةُ فَلَا يَشْتَرِطُ المساواة فيه
He said: If one of them inherits something in which partnership is not valid, or is given as a gift and it reaches his hand, the mufawadah is void and becomes 'inan because equality is lost in what can serve as capital, since it is a condition in it initially and perpetually. This is because the other does not share in what he received due to the absence of a cause in his right. However, it transforms into 'inan for possibility, because equality is not a condition in it, and for its continuance the same rule applies as at inception since it is not binding. If one of them inherits goods, then it is his and the mufawadah is not spoiled; likewise real estate, because partnership is not valid in it, so equality is not a condition therein.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings regarding shirkat al-mufawadah (universal partnership) when one partner acquires new property through inheritance or gift. The first ruling: if the acquired property is of a type in which partnership is valid (i.e., currency or trade goods that can serve as partnership capital), and the partner takes possession, then the mufawadah is void and automatically transforms into shirkat al-'inan (limited partnership). This is because mufawadah requires equality (musawah) in capital both at inception and throughout its duration. The other partner cannot share in this new property due to lack of cause (sabab) — only the recipient has a right to it. Equality is lost, so mufawadah cannot continue. However, since 'inan does not require equality, the contract can continue as 'inan. The second ruling: if the inherited or gifted property is 'arad (goods, non-currency merchandise) or 'iqar (real estate like land or houses), the mufawadah remains valid and does not transform. This is because partnership is not valid in such items — they cannot be capital in a partnership contract. Since they are outside the scope of partnership, equality in them is not a condition. The commentary in Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the reasoning: in mufawadah, partners are agents (wakil) and guarantors (kafil) for each other, and equality in capital that can be partnership property is required. The passage then transitions to clarifying what types of property are eligible for partnership.

Key Terms
شركة المفاوضة (shirkat al-mufawadah): A universal partnership where partners are equal in capital, profit, and loss, and act as agents and guarantors for each other in all permissible transactions.
شركة العنان (shirkat al-'inan): A limited partnership where partners contribute capital and share profit/loss proportionally, but do not assume universal agency or guaranty.
مساواة (musawah): Equality between partners in the capital that forms the basis of the partnership, required in mufawadah at inception and throughout.
عرض ('arad): Non-currency trade goods, such as merchandise or commodities, as opposed to dirhams and dinars.
عقار ('iqar): Real estate such as land and buildings, which cannot be the subject of partnership in the Hanafi view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mufawadah partnership, if one partner inherits a quantity of cloth (goods), what is the ruling?
A) The mufawadah becomes void and becomes 'inan.
B) The mufawadah remains valid as is.
C) The mufawadah is void completely.
D) The cloth becomes partnership property.

Key Takeaway
When a mufawadah partner acquires partnership-eligible property (currency) through inheritance or gift, equality is lost and the contract transforms into 'inan; acquisition of goods or real estate does not affect the partnership.

Capital in Sharikat Mufawadah: The Requirement of Monetary Forms
Establishes a validity condition for sharikat mufawadah with proofs and refutation of Malik

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for capital in sharikat mufawadah and explain the rational proof that disallows commodities.

Core Idea
Sharikat mufawadah requires monetary capital; commodities lead to profit from unguaranteed wealth.

This passage establishes a key condition for the validity of sharikat mufawadah: the capital must be dirhams, dinars, or current coins. Imam Malik's opposing view is presented and refuted. Understanding this condition is crucial to distinguishing Hanafi partnership law from other schools and grasping the principle that profit corresponds to liability.

Guided Path
1
State the act and ruling
Identify that the act is forming a sharikat mufawadah and the ruling is that it is conditional upon capital being dirhams, dinars, or current coins.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that sharikat mufawadah is not valid (fasid) if capital is commodities, measured, or weighed items, even of the same genus.
This is a condition of validity (shart sihha).
3
Locate the opposing view
Imam Malik allows sharikat mufawadah with commodities of the same genus, comparing them to coins because they are known capital.
The opposing school is Maliki.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is rational: if partners sell their respective commodities at different prices, the excess one partner gains is profit from wealth he neither owned nor guaranteed (ribh ma lam yudman). With coins, the price is a debt in the buyer's liability from the moment of purchase, so profit is from guaranteed wealth.
Also, the first transaction in commodities is sale (which cannot be conditional on partnership), while in coins it is purchase (which can).
5
Note the thamarah al‑khilaf
The practical consequence: Maliki school allows sharikat mufawadah with goods of the same genus; Hanafis do not. The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلُّ وَلَا يَنْعَقِدُ الشَّرُكَةُ إِلَّا بِالدَّرَاهِم وَالدَّنَانِيرِ وَالْفُلُوسِ النَّافِقَةِ وَقَالَ مَالِك يَجُوزُ بِالعرُوضِ وَالْمَكِيل وَالْمَوزُونِ إِذَا كَانَ الْجنس واحد الأَنها عُقِدَتْ عَلَى رَأسِ مَالٍ مَعْلَوْمٍ فَاشْبَهَ التَّقْرُدَ بِخِلَافِ الْمُضَارَبَةِ لِأَنَّ الْقِيَاسَ بِأَبَاهَا لِمَا فِيْهَا مِنْ رِبْحِ مَالَمْ يَضْمَنُ فَتَقْتَصِرُ عَلَى مَوْرِدِ الشَّرْعِ وَلَنَا أَنَهُ يُؤَدِى إِلَى رِبْحْ مَالَمْ يَضْمَنْ لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا بَاعَ كُلٌّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا رَأْسَ مَالِهِ وَيُفَاضِلُ الثَّمَنَانِ فَمَايَسْتَحِقُه أَحَدُهُمَا مِنَ الرِّيَادَةِ فِي مَالَ صَاحِبِهِ رِبْحُ مَالَمُ يَمْلِك وَمَالَمْ يَضْمَنْ بِخِلَافِ الدَّرَاهِم وَالدَّنَانِيرِ لِأَنَّ ثَمَنَ مَا يَشْتَرِيهِ فِي ذِمَّتِهِ إِذْهِيَ لَا يَتَعَيَّنُ فَكَانَ رِبَّحَ مَا ضَمِنَ وَلأنَّ أَوَّلَ التَّصَرُّفِ فِي الْعُرُوضِ الْبَيْعُ وَفِي التَّفَوْدِ الشّرَاءُ وَبَيْعُ أَحَدِهِمَا مَالَهُ عَلَى أَنْ يَكُونَ الْأَخَرُ شَرِيكَافِي تميم لَا يَجُوزُ وَشِرَاءُ أَحَدِهِمَا شَيْئًا بِمَالِهِ عَلَى أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَيِيعُ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ غَيْرِهِ جَائِرُ وَأَمَّا الْفُلُوسُ النَّافِقَةُ تَرُوجُ رِوَاجَ الْأَيْمَانِ فَالْحِقَتْ بِهَا قَالُواهَذَا قَوْلُ مُحَمَّدٍ لِأَنَّهَا مُلْحَقَةُ بِالتَّقْوَدِ عِنْدَهُ حَتَّى تى لَا تَتَعينُ بِالتَّعيين وَلَا يَجُوزُ بَيع ثنَيْنِ بِوَاحِدٍ بِأَعْيَانِهَا عَلَى مَاعُرِفَ أَمَّا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِى يُوسُفَ لَا يَجُوزُ الشَّرُكَةُ وَالمُضَارَبَةُ بِهَا لأَنَّ ثَمَنَّيت تَتَبَدَّلُ سَاعَةً فَسَاعَةً وَتَصِيرُ لَعَاوَيُرْوَى عَنْ أَبِي يُوْسُفَ مِثْلَ قَوْلِ مُحَمَّدٍ وَالْأَوَّلُ أَقْيَسُ وَأَظْهَرُ وَ عَنْ أَبِى حَيْقَةٌ صَحَّةُ الْمُضَارَبَةِ بِهَا
Section: Partnership is not valid except with dirhams, dinars, and current coins. Malik said: It is valid with commodities, measured and weighed items if the genus is one, because they are contracted upon known capital, so they resemble coins; unlike mudarabah because analogy rejects it due to profit from unguaranteed wealth, so it is confined to the revealed texts. Our proof: It leads to profit from unguaranteed wealth because when each of them sells his capital and the prices differ, what one of them gains from the excess in his partner's wealth is profit from what he does not own and has not guaranteed. Unlike dirhams and dinars, because the price of what he buys is in his liability since they are not specific, so it is profit from what he guaranteed. Also because the first transaction in commodities is sale, while in coins it is purchase. Selling one's property on condition that the other is a partner in the price is invalid, while buying something with his property on condition that the purchased item is between him and another is valid. As for current coins, they circulate like prices, so they are included with them. They said: This is the statement of Muhammad because they are treated like coins in his view, to the extent that they are not specified by designation, and selling two specific ones for one specific one is not allowed as known. As for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: partnership and mudarabah with them are not valid because their values change from moment to moment and they become like commodities. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf something like Muhammad's statement, but the first is more analogous and apparent. And from Abu Hanifa: mudarabah with them is valid.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on partnership (shirkah). It establishes the condition that sharikat mufawadah (universal partnership) is only valid when the capital consists of dirhams, dinars, or current coins (fulus naafiqah). Imam Malik's opposing view allowing commodities of the same genus is stated and then refuted. The Hanafi proof is rational (ta'lil): if commodities were allowed, each partner might sell his own goods at different prices, and the resulting excess profit would be from wealth he neither owned nor guaranteed (gharar). With coins, the price is a debt in the partner's liability, so profit is from guaranteed wealth. The passage also discusses fulus (copper coins): according to Imam Muhammad, they are treated like dirhams/dinars because they circulate as prices and are not specific in designation. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf initially hold that fulus are not valid because their value fluctuates, though a narration from Abu Yusuf agrees with Muhammad, and another from Abu Hanifa allows mudarabah with them. The relied-upon position (al-awwal aqyas wa azhar) is the stricter view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
شركة مفاوضة (sharikat mufawadah): A partnership of full equality in capital, profit, and agency, where each partner is a surety for the other in all transactions.
فلوس نافقة (fulus naafiqah): Copper or base metal coins that are currently in circulation and accepted as money.
ربح ما لم يضمن (ribh ma lam yudman): Profit from wealth that one has not guaranteed; considered impermissible because profit corresponds to liability (الخراج بالضمان).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof for disallowing commodities as capital in sharikat mufawadah?
A) Commodities are not known to the partners.
B) It leads to profit from unguaranteed wealth.
C) The Quran explicitly prohibits it.
D) Ijma of the companions.

Key Takeaway
Sharikat mufawadah is valid only with monetary capital; commodities lead to prohibited profit from unguaranteed wealth.

Intra-Hanafi Difference on Current Coins (Fulus) as Capital
Discusses the intra-Hanafi difference on using fulus as capital for sharikat mufawadah and mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the views of Imam Muhammad, Abu Hanifa, and Abu Yusuf regarding fulus as capital in sharikat mufawadah and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Imam Muhammad allows fulus; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf disallow, and the latter view is relied upon.

After establishing the general condition, the passage turns to the specific case of fulus (copper coins). The three founding imams of the Hanafi school differed on whether fulus are considered monetary capital. This lesson unpacks that intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and explains why the stricter view prevails.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi condition
Recall that sharikat mufawadah requires capital to be dirhams, dinars, or current coins (fulus naafiqah).
2
Identify the specific issue: fulus
Fulus are low-value coins that circulate. The question is whether they qualify as 'current coins'.
Fulus naafiqah are those in circulation.
3
Present Imam Muhammad's position
Imam Muhammad considers fulus equivalent to dirhams/dinars because they circulate as prices and are not specific in designation (ghayr muta'ayyin).
His reasoning: fulus are like prices themselves.
4
Present Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's position
They argue fulus are not valid for partnership or mudarabah because their values fluctuate constantly, making them akin to commodities.
A narration from Abu Yusuf agrees with Muhammad, but the stronger view is the first.
5
Note the mufta bihi and the exception for mudarabah
The relied-upon position is the stricter one (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). However, a narration from Abu Hanifa allows mudarabah with fulus, showing an exception.
The commentator states: 'الأوّل أقيس وأظهر' (the first is more analogous and apparent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلُّ وَلَا يَنْعَقِدُ الشَّرُكَةُ إِلَّا بِالدَّرَاهِم وَالدَّنَانِيرِ وَالْفُلُوسِ النَّافِقَةِ وَقَالَ مَالِك يَجُوزُ بِالعرُوضِ وَالْمَكِيل وَالْمَوزُونِ إِذَا كَانَ الْجنس واحد الأَنها عُقِدَتْ عَلَى رَأسِ مَالٍ مَعْلَوْمٍ فَاشْبَهَ التَّقْرُدَ بِخِلَافِ الْمُضَارَبَةِ لِأَنَّ الْقِيَاسَ بِأَبَاهَا لِمَا فِيْهَا مِنْ رِبْحِ مَالَمْ يَضْمَنُ فَتَقْتَصِرُ عَلَى مَوْرِدِ الشَّرْعِ وَلَنَا أَنَهُ يُؤَدِى إِلَى رِبْحْ مَالَمْ يَضْمَنْ لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا بَاعَ كُلٌّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا رَأْسَ مَالِهِ وَيُفَاضِلُ الثَّمَنَانِ فَمَايَسْتَحِقُه أَحَدُهُمَا مِنَ الرِّيَادَةِ فِي مَالَ صَاحِبِهِ رِبْحُ مَالَمُ يَمْلِك وَمَالَمْ يَضْمَنْ بِخِلَافِ الدَّرَاهِم وَالدَّنَانِيرِ لِأَنَّ ثَمَنَ مَا يَشْتَرِيهِ فِي ذِمَّتِهِ إِذْهِيَ لَا يَتَعَيَّنُ فَكَانَ رِبَّحَ مَا ضَمِنَ وَلأنَّ أَوَّلَ التَّصَرُّفِ فِي الْعُرُوضِ الْبَيْعُ وَفِي التَّفَوْدِ الشّرَاءُ وَبَيْعُ أَحَدِهِمَا مَالَهُ عَلَى أَنْ يَكُونَ الْأَخَرُ شَرِيكَافِي تميم لَا يَجُوزُ وَشِرَاءُ أَحَدِهِمَا شَيْئًا بِمَالِهِ عَلَى أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمَيِيعُ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ غَيْرِهِ جَائِرُ وَأَمَّا الْفُلُوسُ النَّافِقَةُ تَرُوجُ رِوَاجَ الْأَيْمَانِ فَالْحِقَتْ بِهَا قَالُواهَذَا قَوْلُ مُحَمَّدٍ لِأَنَّهَا مُلْحَقَةُ بِالتَّقْوَدِ عِنْدَهُ حَتَّى تى لَا تَتَعينُ بِالتَّعيين وَلَا يَجُوزُ بَيع ثنَيْنِ بِوَاحِدٍ بِأَعْيَانِهَا عَلَى مَاعُرِفَ أَمَّا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِى يُوسُفَ لَا يَجُوزُ الشَّرُكَةُ وَالمُضَارَبَةُ بِهَا لأَنَّ ثَمَنَّيت تَتَبَدَّلُ سَاعَةً فَسَاعَةً وَتَصِيرُ لَعَاوَيُرْوَى عَنْ أَبِي يُوْسُفَ مِثْلَ قَوْلِ مُحَمَّدٍ وَالْأَوَّلُ أَقْيَسُ وَأَظْهَرُ وَ عَنْ أَبِى حَيْقَةٌ صَحَّةُ الْمُضَارَبَةِ بِهَا
Section: Partnership is not valid except with dirhams, dinars, and current coins. Malik said: It is valid with commodities, measured and weighed items if the genus is one, because they are contracted upon known capital, so they resemble coins; unlike mudarabah because analogy rejects it due to profit from unguaranteed wealth, so it is confined to the revealed texts. Our proof: It leads to profit from unguaranteed wealth because when each of them sells his capital and the prices differ, what one of them gains from the excess in his partner's wealth is profit from what he does not own and has not guaranteed. Unlike dirhams and dinars, because the price of what he buys is in his liability since they are not specific, so it is profit from what he guaranteed. Also because the first transaction in commodities is sale, while in coins it is purchase. Selling one's property on condition that the other is a partner in the price is invalid, while buying something with his property on condition that the purchased item is between him and another is valid. As for current coins, they circulate like prices, so they are included with them. They said: This is the statement of Muhammad because they are treated like coins in his view, to the extent that they are not specified by designation, and selling two specific ones for one specific one is not allowed as known. As for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: partnership and mudarabah with them are not valid because their values change from moment to moment and they become like commodities. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf something like Muhammad's statement, but the first is more analogous and apparent. And from Abu Hanifa: mudarabah with them is valid.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on partnership (shirkah). It establishes the condition that sharikat mufawadah (universal partnership) is only valid when the capital consists of dirhams, dinars, or current coins (fulus naafiqah). Imam Malik's opposing view allowing commodities of the same genus is stated and then refuted. The Hanafi proof is rational (ta'lil): if commodities were allowed, each partner might sell his own goods at different prices, and the resulting excess profit would be from wealth he neither owned nor guaranteed (gharar). With coins, the price is a debt in the partner's liability, so profit is from guaranteed wealth. The passage also discusses fulus (copper coins): according to Imam Muhammad, they are treated like dirhams/dinars because they circulate as prices and are not specific in designation. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf initially hold that fulus are not valid because their value fluctuates, though a narration from Abu Yusuf agrees with Muhammad, and another from Abu Hanifa allows mudarabah with them. The relied-upon position (al-awwal aqyas wa azhar) is the stricter view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
شركة مفاوضة (sharikat mufawadah): A partnership of full equality in capital, profit, and agency, where each partner is a surety for the other in all transactions.
فلوس نافقة (fulus naafiqah): Copper or base metal coins that are currently in circulation and accepted as money.
مفاوضة (mufawadah): Type of partnership with full agency and suretyship; also the form of partnership discussed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, is sharikat mufawadah valid using fulus naafiqah?
A) Yes, because they are current coins.
B) No, because their values fluctuate.
C) Yes, but only with Muhammad's view.
D) No, because they are not made of silver or gold.

Key Takeaway
Fulus naafiqah are not valid capital for sharikat mufawadah per the mufta bihi, despite a dissenting view from Imam Muhammad.

Partnership with Tibr and Nuqrah: Exception by Custom
States an exception by custom for uncoined gold and silver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for partnership capital and identify when tibr and nuqrah are valid due to custom.

Core Idea
Custom (ta'amul) can elevate uncoined gold and silver to valid partnership capital.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on partnership capital. The general principle is that only coined money (dirhams and dinars) is valid for sharikat mufawadah. However, a key exception is made for uncoined gold (tibr) and uncoined silver (nuqrah) if people customarily transact with them. This exception highlights the role of custom ('urf) in modifying default fiqh rules.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Recall that sharikat mufawadah is not valid with any property other than dirhams, dinars, and current coins. This is the default position.
This is established in Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar.
2
Identify the exception
The text says 'unless people customarily deal with tibr and nuqrah'. Identify that custom creates an exception.
The condition is 'ta'amul' (customary dealing).
3
Understand the condition
Custom must be widespread among people in that locality or market. Not mere occasional use.
This is a conditional permission, not unconditional.
4
Note the internal difference
The passage notes a difference between narrations regarding the legal nature of tibr and nuqrah. This does not affect the exception if custom exists.
The commentary resolves by prioritizing custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَايَجُوزُ الشِّرْكَةُ بِمَاسِوى ذَالِكَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَتَعَامَلَ النَّاسُ بِالتَّبْرِ وَ التَّقْوَرَةِ فَتَصِحُ الشَّرُكَةُ بِهِمَا هُكَدَادَ كَرَ فِي الكِتاب وفِى الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ وَلَا يَكُونُ الْمُفَاوَضَةُ بِمَثَاقِيلِ ذَهَبِ أَوْ فِضَّةٍ وَمُرَادُهُ التبرُ فَعَلَى هَذِهِ الرِّوَايَةِ اليَبْرُ سِلْعَةُ من بِالتَّعَينَ فَلَا يَصْلَحُ رَأْسَ الْمَالِ فِى الْمُضَارَبَاتِ وَالشَّرَكَاتِ وَذَكَرَ فِى كِتَابِ الصَّرْفِ أَنَّ التَّقْرَةَ لَا يَتَعَين لَا يَنفَسخُ الْعَقْدُ بِهَلَاكِ، قَبْلَ التَّسْلِيمِ فَعَلَى تِلْكَ الرِّوَايَةِ يَصْلَحُ رَاسَ الْمَالِ فِيْهِمَا وَهَذَا لِمَاعُرِفَ أَنَّهُمَا
He said: Partnership with other than that is not valid unless people deal with tibr (uncoined gold) and nuqrah (uncoined silver), then partnership with them is valid. Thus it is mentioned in the book. And in Al-Jami' al-Saghir: Mufawadah is not with mithqals of gold or silver, and his intent is tibr. So according to this narration, tibr is a commodity subject to specification, so it is not suitable as capital in mudarabahs and partnerships. And he mentioned in Kitab al-Sarf that nuqrah is not specific, the contract is not dissolved by its destruction before delivery. So according to that narration, it is suitable as capital in both. This is because they are known.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from Al-Quduri's text, with commentary. The general rule is that sharikat mufawadah is not valid with anything other than dirhams, dinars, and current coins. However, an exception is made for tibr (uncoined gold) and nuqrah (uncoined silver) if people customarily use them in transactions (ta'amul). This is stated in Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The passage then notes an apparent conflict with Al-Jami' al-Saghir, which says mufawadah is not valid with mithqals of gold or silver (meaning tibr). The resolution: according to one narration, tibr is a specific commodity (silah), so it cannot be capital; but according to another narration (from Kitab al-Sarf), nuqrah is not specific (it is like coins in being non-specific), so it can be capital. The commentator notes that this difference arises because tibr and nuqrah have different legal treatments depending on how they are used in custom and contract.

Key Terms
تبر (tibr): Uncoined gold, in the form of dust, nuggets, or ingots, before being minted into coins.
نقرة (nuqrah): Uncoined silver, melted or in raw form, not yet struck into coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Quduri's text, what condition allows sharikat mufawadah to be valid with uncoined gold (tibr) and silver (nuqrah)?
A) They must be weighed exactly.
B) People must customarily deal with them.
C) They must be melted into bars.
D) They must be minted as coins first.

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) can override the default rule that partnership capital must be coined money, making tibr and nuqrah valid when people transact with them.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Is Tibr a Specific Commodity?
Explains the internal Hanafi difference regarding tibr and nuqrah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the internal Hanafi difference on the legal nature of tibr and nuqrah and identify the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Tibr is treated as a specific commodity in one narration, nuqrah as non-specific in another; custom resolves the conflict.

This lesson delves into the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf mentioned in the passage. While the general rule with custom is clear, there is a scholarly difference about the inherent legal classification of tibr and nuqrah. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate conflicting narrations within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
One narration from Al-Jami' al-Saghir treats tibr as a specific commodity (ma'rifah bi al-'ayn). Another from Kitab al-Sarf treats nuqrah as non-specific.
The source says 'tibr' is intended when mithqals of gold are mentioned.
2
Analyze the basis of each
Tibr is specific because it is a measurable commodity with individual identity. Nuqrah is non-specific because it is like molten silver, treated as fungible.
This affects contract dissolution on destruction before delivery.
3
Determine the practical consequence
If specific, tibr cannot be capital in partnerships. If non-specific, nuqrah can be capital. The difference matters when custom is absent.
The custom exception (ta'amul) overrides these classifications.
4
Note the mufta bihi
The commentator does not explicitly tarjih, but the passage implies that custom is the decisive factor. In practice, fatwa is given based on custom.
This shows the flexibility of the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَايَجُوزُ الشِّرْكَةُ بِمَاسِوى ذَالِكَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَتَعَامَلَ النَّاسُ بِالتَّبْرِ وَ التَّقْوَرَةِ فَتَصِحُ الشَّرُكَةُ بِهِمَا هُكَدَادَ كَرَ فِي الكِتاب وفِى الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيرِ وَلَا يَكُونُ الْمُفَاوَضَةُ بِمَثَاقِيلِ ذَهَبِ أَوْ فِضَّةٍ وَمُرَادُهُ التبرُ فَعَلَى هَذِهِ الرِّوَايَةِ اليَبْرُ سِلْعَةُ من بِالتَّعَينَ فَلَا يَصْلَحُ رَأْسَ الْمَالِ فِى الْمُضَارَبَاتِ وَالشَّرَكَاتِ وَذَكَرَ فِى كِتَابِ الصَّرْفِ أَنَّ التَّقْرَةَ لَا يَتَعَين لَا يَنفَسخُ الْعَقْدُ بِهَلَاكِ، قَبْلَ التَّسْلِيمِ فَعَلَى تِلْكَ الرِّوَايَةِ يَصْلَحُ رَاسَ الْمَالِ فِيْهِمَا وَهَذَا لِمَاعُرِفَ أَنَّهُمَا
He said: Partnership with other than that is not valid unless people deal with tibr (uncoined gold) and nuqrah (uncoined silver), then partnership with them is valid. Thus it is mentioned in the book. And in Al-Jami' al-Saghir: Mufawadah is not with mithqals of gold or silver, and his intent is tibr. So according to this narration, tibr is a commodity subject to specification, so it is not suitable as capital in mudarabahs and partnerships. And he mentioned in Kitab al-Sarf that nuqrah is not specific, the contract is not dissolved by its destruction before delivery. So according to that narration, it is suitable as capital in both. This is because they are known.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from Al-Quduri's text, with commentary. The general rule is that sharikat mufawadah is not valid with anything other than dirhams, dinars, and current coins. However, an exception is made for tibr (uncoined gold) and nuqrah (uncoined silver) if people customarily use them in transactions (ta'amul). This is stated in Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. The passage then notes an apparent conflict with Al-Jami' al-Saghir, which says mufawadah is not valid with mithqals of gold or silver (meaning tibr). The resolution: according to one narration, tibr is a specific commodity (silah), so it cannot be capital; but according to another narration (from Kitab al-Sarf), nuqrah is not specific (it is like coins in being non-specific), so it can be capital. The commentator notes that this difference arises because tibr and nuqrah have different legal treatments depending on how they are used in custom and contract.

Key Terms
تبر (tibr): Uncoined gold, in the form of dust, nuggets, or ingots, before being minted into coins.
نقرة (nuqrah): Uncoined silver, melted or in raw form, not yet struck into coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration from Al-Jami' al-Saghir, why is tibr not suitable as capital in partnerships?
A) Because it is not minted
B) Because it is a specific commodity (silah bi al-ta'ayyun)
C) Because it is too heavy to weigh
D) Because gold is always invalid

Key Takeaway

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Validity of Partnership with Fulūs
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fulūs as capital in partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the validity of shirkat al-mufawada and mudaraba with fulus, explain the talil behind each, and identify which position is favored in the passage.

Core Idea
Fulūs are either cash (thaman) or goods (sil'a)—the core dispute determining partnership validity.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a classical intra-Hanafi disagreement about using copper coins (fulūs) as capital in partnership. The dispute turns on a single question: Are fulūs treated like dirhams and dinars (thaman) or like ordinary goods? Imām Muhammad argues they are cash, while Abū Hanīfa and Abū Yūsuf classify them as goods lacking intrinsic value. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to trace the illa (cause) behind Hanafi rulings and to see how later scholars weighed conflicting analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Imām Muhammad's view and the view of Abū Hanīfa & Abū Yūsuf regarding the validity of partnership with fulūs.
Muhammad: valid; Abū Hanīfa & Abū Yūsuf: invalid.
2
Locate the dalil for each
Muhammad argues fulūs are non-specified (ghayr muta‘ayyin) like dirhams/dinars and circulate as cash. The elders argue fulūs have no intrinsic value (lā thamanīyah la-hā), so they become goods when banned.
The illa for Muhammad: function as currency; for elders: lack of intrinsic value.
3
Follow the talil
Explain the reasoning: In Muhammad's view, fulūs are treated as thaman because they are current and non-specified; in the elders' view, they are sil'a because their value is purely conventional and they can be demonetized.
This is a classic example of ta'lil bi al-‘illa.
4
Note the riwayah variants
Identify the alternative narrations: Abū Yūsuf has a second narration agreeing with Muhammad; Abū Hanīfa also has a narration permitting mudaraba with fulūs.
These show the ikhtilaf is not absolute; later practice may adopt the permissive view.
5
Determine the tarjih
The text states that the view of Abū Hanīfa and Abū Yūsuf is 'closer to qiyas and more apparent' (zāhir). Understand that the commentator favors this view, but acknowledge the existence of conflicting evidence.
Tarjih is based on strength of analogy and apparent meaning, not just number of narrators.

خریدنے میں مسئلہ متعین نہیں ہوتا ہے اس لئے یہ ایسے مال کا نفع ہوا جو ضمانت میں آچکا ہے۔ اور اس لئے بھی کہ اسباب میں پہلا تصرف یہ ہے کہ فروخت کرے۔ اور نقود میں پہلا تصرف یہ ہے کہ خرید ہے۔ حالانکہ عقد مفاوضہ میں شریک شخص کا اپنا مال اس شرط پر فروخت کرنا ہے کہ دوسرا بھی اس کی قیمت میں شریک ہو، جائز نہیں ہے اور ایک شخص کا اپنے مال سے کوئی چیز اس شرط پر خرید نا کہ اس کا دوسرا شریک بھی اس میں شریک ہو یعنی بیچ دونوں میں مشترک ہو یہ جائز ہے ( اس طرح نقود کے سوا دوسرے اسباب سے جائز نہیں ہوگا اور درہم و دینار کے علاوہ وہ سکے جو رائج الوقت ہوں تو وہ بھی اشرفی اور روپے کی طرح چلتے ہیں۔ لہذا وہ بھی نقد میں شامل کر لئے گئے ۔ اور متاخر ی نا مشائخ رحمتہ اللہ علیہم نے فرمایا ہے کہ یہ قول امام محمد رحمتہ اللہ علیہ کا ہے کیونکہ ان کے نزدیک فلوس بھی نقد کے ساتھ ملالئے گئے ہیں ۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ جس طرح درہم و دینار متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتے ہیں اسی طرح سے یہ فلوس بھی متعین نہیں ہوتے ہیں۔ (مثلا کسی نے چار آنے کی کوئی چیز خریدی تو اسے یہ اختیار ہوگا کہ چاہے وہی چار آنے دے جو خریدتے وقت ہاتھ میں دکھائے تھے یا اس کو چھوڑ کر دوسرے پیسے دے دے۔ کیونکہ متعین کرنے کے باوجود یہ متعین نہیں ہوتے ہیں۔ ) اور معین دو پیسوں کو دوسرے معین ایک پیسہ سے بدلنا اور بیچنا بھی جائز نہیں ہے۔ جیسا کہ اصول میں معلوم ہو چکا ہے لہذا اسے معین کرنا بے فائدہ ہوگا) اور امام ابوحنیفہ وابو یوسف رحمہما اللہ سکے نزدیک پیسوں سے نہ شرکت کا معاملہ صحیح ہوگا اور نہ مضاربت صحیح ہو گی ۔ کیونکہ ان پیسوں اور فلوس کی خود کوئی قیمت نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ اسی لئے جب ان کو بند کر دیا جائے یا ان پر پابندی عائد کر دی جائے تو وہ بھی اسباب کے حکم میں آ جاتے ہیں اور ابو یوسف رحمتہ اللہ علیہ سے دوسری بروایت مثل امام محمد رحمہ اللہ علیہ کے بھی مذکور ہے۔ لیکن ان کا ابوحنیفہ رحمہ اللہ علیہ کے ساتھ ہونا قیاس کے زیادہ قریب اور زیادہ ظاہر ہے۔ اور ابوحنیفہ رحمتہ اللہ علیہ سے بھی یہ روایت مذکور ہے کہ فلوس کے ساتھ مضاربت صحیح ہے
In purchasing, the specific item is not determined (by specification), so this is a profit on wealth that has come under liability. And also because among assets, the first transaction is sale, and among currencies, the first transaction is purchase. While in the contract of mufawada, it is not valid for a partner to sell his own property on condition that the other partner shares in its price. But it is valid for a person to buy something from his own property on condition that his other partner also shares in it, i.e., the purchase becomes common between them. (Thus, besides cash, it is not valid with other assets. And besides dirhams and dinars, coins that are current are also treated like gold coins and rupees. Therefore, they are included in cash. And the later scholars (muta'akhkhirīn mashāyikh) said that this is the view of Imām Muḥammad, because in his view fulūs are also included with cash. This is why just as dirhams and dinars are not specified by marking, similarly these fulūs are not specified. For example, if someone bought something for four ānas, he has the option to give the same four ānas he showed at the time of purchase or to give other coins instead, because despite marking they are not specific. And it is not valid to exchange specific two paisas for another specific one paisa, as known from principles. Thus specifying them is useless.) And according to Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and Imām Abū Yūsuf (Allāh have mercy on them), neither will the transaction of partnership with coins be valid nor will muḍāraba be valid, because these coins and fulūs have no intrinsic value. Therefore, when they are discontinued or banned, they come under the ruling of goods. And from Abū Yūsuf there is another narration like Imām Muḥammad's view. But his agreement with Abū Ḥanīfa is closer to analogy and more apparent. And from Abū Ḥanīfa also a narration is transmitted that muḍāraba with fulūs is valid.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of using fulūs (copper coins) as capital in Shirkat al-Mufāwaḍa (unlimited partnership) and Muḍāraba (profit-sharing partnership). It begins by contrasting the treatment of cash (nuqūd) and goods (‘urūḍ) in the context of individual transactions by partners. In Mufāwaḍa, selling one's own property on condition that the other partner shares the price is invalid, but buying on that condition is valid. This is because in goods the primary transaction is sale, while in cash it is purchase. However, this ruling applies only to cash; other assets are not included. The text then clarifies that besides dirhams and dinars, other current coins (like Ashrafis and rupees) are treated as cash. The later scholars (muta'akhkhirūn) attribute this inclusive view to Imām Muḥammad, who regards fulūs as cash because they are not specified (ghayr muta‘ayyin) like dirhams and dinars; specifying them is futile, and one may substitute them. Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf hold that neither partnership nor muḍāraba is valid with fulūs because they have no intrinsic value (thamanīyah) and are thus like goods; if they are banned, they become contingent goods. However, there is a narration from Abū Yūsuf agreeing with Muḥammad, but the text considers Abū Yūsuf's agreement with Abū Ḥanīfa to be stronger analogically. Another narration from Abū Ḥanīfa itself permits muḍāraba with fulūs. Thus multiple positions exist, but the relied-upon (muftā bihī) view likely follows the majority of the two elders.

Key Terms
فلوس (fulūs): Copper coins used as currency, but without intrinsic metallic value like gold or silver.
مفاوضہ (mufāwaḍa): A type of partnership where partners have equal shares in capital, profit, and authority.
مضاربت (muḍāraba): A profit-sharing partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and Imām Abū Yūsuf consider partnership with fulūs invalid?
A) Fulūs are not minted by the state.
B) Fulūs have no intrinsic value and become goods when banned.
C) Fulūs are not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Fulūs cannot be weighed or measured.

Key Takeaway
The validity of partnership with fulūs hinges on whether they are classified as thaman (currency) or sil'a (goods), a core intra-Hanafi dispute with practical implications for modern currencies.

Shirkah — Partnership

The Role of Custom in Partnership with Uncoined Gold and Silver
Fiqh ruling on capital in Mufāwaḍa with custom exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on using tibr and naqrah as capital in Mufāwaḍa and explain how custom (ta‘āmul) can override the default ruling.

Core Idea
Custom makes uncoined gold/silver valid as capital despite their default commodity status.

This passage from Qudūrī and Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr addresses a practical question: can partners contribute uncoined gold or silver to a Mufāwaḍa partnership? The default Hanafi ruling treats such items as commodities (sil‘a), not currency (thaman), but a key exception based on custom emerges. By analyzing the two narrations from Imām Muḥammad, you will see how the school balances textual authority with local practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Rule
Begin with al-Qudūrī's statement: partnership is not valid with other than the specified types (coined dirhams/dinars) unless people customarily deal with tibr and naqrah. This establishes a default of invalidity.
The default is based on the principle that capital must be thaman (currency), and uncoined metals are normally commodities.
2
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Examine the two narrations from Imām Muḥammad: (1) In Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr, tibr is a commodity that becomes specified by identification, thus invalid as capital. (2) In Kitāb al-Ṣarf, naqrah does not become specified, so it can be capital. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The difference hinges on whether uncoined metals are considered specified (mut‘ayyan) or not.
3
Apply the Custom Exception
Note the exception clause (إلا أن يتعامَل الناس). If a community customarily treats tibr/naqrah as a medium of exchange, they acquire the ruling of thaman. This overrides the default and makes partnership valid.
Custom (‘urf) is a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school, capable of changing a ruling.
4
Determine the Muftā Bihi
The commentator prefers the first narration (Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr) as aṣaḥ, meaning the default is invalidity. However, if custom exists, that position is set aside. Thus the operative ruling depends on prevailing practice.
The tarjih is based on the principle that uncoined metals are primarily commodities, and their use as currency is secondary.
